Permissible Minor Actions in Prayer: Wiping, Looking, Shaking Garment
Lists permissible minor actions with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which minor actions are permissible during Hanafi prayer and identify the condition of 'amal qalil (small action).

Core Idea
Small necessary movements do not break prayer; permission is tied to need and minimal action.

The Hanafi school recognizes that prayer requires focus, but also accommodates human necessity. This passage details several actions—shaking the garment, wiping the forehead, and looking around—that do not invalidate the prayer when done for a valid reason and with minimal movement. It also distinguishes between actions performed during and after prayer, and encourages leaving them when unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify each permissible action
Read through the text and list the three main actions: shaking the garment (نفض الثوب), wiping the forehead (مسح الجبهة), and looking with the corners of the eyes (النظر بمؤخرة العينين).
Each action has a specific rationale: avoiding limb outlines, removing dust/grass, avoiding distraction.
2
Note the condition for each action
Identify the common condition: the action must be 'amal qalil (small or minimal) and must serve a legitimate need (e.g., preventing garment from sticking, cleaning for appearance, relieving distraction from sweat).
The phrase 'بعمل قليل' appears explicitly for shaking the garment.
3
Distinguish timing (during vs. after prayer)
Observe that wiping the forehead is allowed both after prayer (for cleanliness) and during prayer only if it harms or distracts (e.g., sweat). This shows a hierarchy: after prayer is routine; during prayer requires a stronger need.
The text says 'ولا بأس بمسحه قبل الفراغ من الصلاة إذا ضره أو شغله عن خشوع الصلاة'.
4
Recognize the preferred avoidance
Note that for looking, the text says 'والأولى تركه لغير حاجة'—leaving it is better if there is no need. This highlights the principle that even permissible actions should be minimized in prayer.
This aligns with the general Hanafi preference to avoid unnecessary movements in prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بأس بنفض ثوبه بعمل قليل كي لا يلتصق بجسده في الركوع تحاشياً عن ظهور صورة الأعضاء، ولا بأس بصونه عن التراب ولا بأس بمسح جبهته من التراب أو الحشيش بعد الفراغ من الصلاة تنظيفاً عن صفة المثلة والملوث ( ولا بأس بمسحه قبل الفراغ من الصلاة إذا ضره أو شغله عن خشوع الصلاة مثل العرق (ولا) بأس (بالنظر بموق عينيه) يمنة ويسرة (من غير تحويل الوجه والأولى تركه لغير حاجة لما فيه من ترك الأدب بالنظر عند
And there is no harm in shaking his garment with a small action so that it does not stick to his body during bowing, avoiding the appearance of limb outlines. And there is no harm in protecting it from dust. And there is no harm in wiping his forehead from dust or grass after finishing the prayer, as a cleansing from the appearance of disfigurement and soiling. And there is no harm in wiping it before finishing the prayer if it harms him or distracts him from humility in prayer, like sweat. And there is no harm in looking with the corners of his eyes to the right and left without turning the face, but leaving it is better for other than need, because it involves abandoning good manners in looking at
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage lists several minor actions that are permissible during prayer, provided they are done with a small amount of action. Shaking one's garment to prevent it from sticking during bowing is allowed to avoid revealing the shape of the body. Protecting the garment from dust is also permissible. Wiping the forehead from dust or grass after finishing the prayer is allowed for cleanliness. If sweat or something else disturbs the worshipper during prayer, wiping the forehead before finishing is permitted if it harms or distracts. Looking around with the eyes without turning the head is allowed, but it is better to avoid unnecessary looking because it contradicts proper etiquette. These rulings reflect the Hanafi principle that necessary or small actions do not invalidate prayer, but excessive or unnecessary actions are disliked.

Key Terms
عمل قليل ('amal qalil): A small amount of physical action that does not invalidate prayer, typically one or two movements.
المثلة (al-muthla): Disfigurement or unsightly appearance; wiping the forehead removes dust that might look unpleasant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is it permissible to wipe one's forehead before finishing the prayer?
A) At any time to look clean.
B) Only after finishing the prayer.
C) If sweat or dust harms or distracts from humility.
D) It is never permissible before finishing.

Key Takeaway
Small permissible actions are allowed in Hanafi prayer when done for necessity and with minimal movement.

Ruling on Prayer Surfaces and Repeating Surah in Nafl
Ruling on prayer surfaces and surah repetition in nafl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prayer surfaces (rugs vs. ground) and repeating a surah in nafl, and distinguish between permissible and preferred.

Core Idea
Prayer on rugs is valid; the best is the ground for humility; repeating a surah in nafl is permissible because its door is wider.

This passage from a later Hanafi source clarifies two common issues: whether it is valid to pray on floor coverings like rugs and carpets, and whether one may repeat the same surah in supererogatory prayers. The author affirms both are permissible, but recommends direct contact with the ground for greater humility. Understanding these nuances helps avoid unnecessary restrictions and aligns practice with the school's emphasis on ease within the bounds of the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts discussed
The passage covers two distinct rulings: (1) the surface on which prayer is performed, and (2) the repetition of a surah in the two rak'ahs of nafl prayer. Recognize each as separate issues within fiqh al-salah.
Acts: prayer surface (rugs/carpets vs. ground) and recitation in nafl.
2
Determine the hukm level for each
For prayer on rugs, the text says 'لا بأس' (no harm), indicating it is mubah (permissible). For direct ground, it says 'الأفضل' (the best), implying mustahabb (recommended). For repeating surah, again 'لا بأس' – permissible.
Hukm levels: mubah for rugs and repetition; mustahabb for ground.
3
Identify the preferred (afdal) and its basis
The preferred surface is the naked ground or natural materials (mats, grass) because they are closer to humility (tawadu‘). The basis is a spiritual virtue, not a condition of validity.
Humility is the key reason for preference.
4
Locate the proof cited for surah repetition
The author points to the prophetic practice: 'he stood with one verse repeating it in his night vigil.' This hadith shows permissibility; the principle 'Babu al-nafl awsa‘' (the door of nafl is wider) supports leniency.
Dalil: Prophet's action in tahajjud.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Maliki school considers prostration on materials like wool, cotton, or linen disliked (makruh) if avoidable. The Hanafi school does not share this dislike.
Practical consequence: Hanafis have more flexibility in mosque flooring.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بأس بالصلاة على الفرش والبسط واللبود إذا وجد الأرض ولا بوضع خرقة يسجد عليها اتقاء الحر والبرد والخشونة الضارة (والأفضل الصلاة على الأرض) بلا حائل أو على ما تنبته كالحصير والحشيش في المساجد وهو أولى من البسط لقربه من التواضع ولا بأس بتكرار السورة في الركعتين من النفل لأن باب النفل أوسع، وقد ورد أنه قام بآية واحدة يكررها في تهجده
And there is no harm in praying on rugs, carpets, and felts if one finds the ground (present), nor in placing a cloth to prostrate on out of fear of heat, cold, and harmful roughness. (The best is prayer on the ground) without a barrier, or on what grows from it like mats and grass in mosques, and it is better than carpets because it is closer to humility. And there is no harm in repeating a surah in the two rak'ahs of supererogatory prayer because the door of supererogation is wider; and it has been reported that he (the Prophet) stood with one verse repeating it in his night vigil.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage provides rulings on the place of prostration and the recitation in supererogatory prayers. It states that it is permissible to pray on rugs, carpets, or felt mats even when the ground is accessible, as long as one is not placing a separation without reason. Placing a cloth to prostrate on for protection from heat, cold, or harsh ground is also allowed. However, the best (afdal) is to pray directly on the ground or on natural materials like grass mats, as this demonstrates greater humility. The passage also permits repeating the same surah in the two rak'ahs of nafl prayer, as the rules for supererogatory acts are more lenient. This is based on the prophetic practice, as reported in hadith, of the Prophet repeating a single verse during his night prayers.

Key Terms
النفل (al-nafl): Supererogatory (voluntary) prayer, as opposed to obligatory (fard) prayer.
التواضع (al-tawadu‘): Humility before Allah; praying directly on the ground is closer to this virtue.
الفرش (al-furush): Rugs or floor coverings used for prayer.
البسط (al-busut): Carpets or spreads.
الحصير (al-hasir): A woven mat, often from palm leaves or reeds; considered a natural surface.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Carpets and rugs
B) The ground or natural materials like grass mats
C) A cloth placed for prostration
D) Felts and wool mats

Key Takeaway
Prayer on rugs and repeating surah in nafl are permissible; direct ground is preferred for humility.

When Cutting Prayer is Obligatory: Helping a Distressed Person
Establishes obligation to break prayer to assist a person in distress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on cutting obligatory prayer to help a distressed person and distinguish it from a parent's call without distress.

Core Idea
Saving a life is wajib even if it means cutting an obligatory prayer.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the later Hanafi tradition: it is obligatory (wajib) to cut one's prayer, even a fard salah, to assist a person in genuine distress who cries for help. This reflects the principle that preserving life (hifz al-nafs) takes precedence over the continuity of prayer. However, this obligation does not extend to a parent's mere call without distress. The passage clarifies the boundary between obligation and permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Cutting an obligatory prayer is ruled wajib under certain conditions.
Note the term 'wajib' – it is obligatory, not merely permissible.
2
Identify the triggering condition
The distressed person must cry for help (istighatha) and you must be able to help.
The Arabic term used is 'istighatha' – a cry for rescue.
3
Recognize the examples of distress
The text gives examples: attacked by oppressor, drowning, animal attack.
These are life-threatening situations.
4
Contrast with the parent's call
A parent's call without distress does not obligate cutting prayer.
This shows the ruling is specific to emergency, not general authority.
5
Understand the underlying principle
Preserving life takes precedence over ritual continuity.
This is consistent with the maqasid al-shari'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يوجب قطع الصلاة وما يجيزه وغير ذلك من تأخير الصلاة وتركها (يجب قطع الصلاة ولو فرضاً (باستغاثة) شخص (ملهوف) لمهم أصابه كما لو تعلق به ظالم أو وقع في ماء أوصال عليه حيوان فاستغاث (بالمصلي) أو بغيره وقدر على الدفع عنه ولا يجب قطع الصلاة (بنداء أحد أبويه) من غير استغاثة
Chapter on what necessitates cutting prayer and what permits it, and other matters of delaying and leaving prayer. (It is obligatory to cut prayer, even if obligatory, by the cry for help of a distressed person for a matter that befell him, such as if a wrongdoer has seized him, or he has fallen into water, or an animal has attacked him, and he cries for help (to the praying person) or to someone else, and he is able to repel it. And cutting prayer is not obligated by the call of one of his parents without a cry for help.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section on the rulings of cutting prayer. It establishes that cutting prayer, even an obligatory prayer (fard), is obligatory (wajib) if a distressed person calls for help and the person praying is able to provide that help. Examples include being attacked by an oppressor, drowning, or being assailed by an animal. However, if one's parent calls without a distress situation, cutting prayer is not obligatory. This distinction is based on the urgency of the situation: saving life takes precedence over prayer. The passage also notes that the ruling applies even if the caller is not directly addressing the person praying but is heard nearby.

Key Terms
الملهوف (al-malhuf): A person in severe distress who cries for help.
الاستغاثة (al-istighatha): A cry for help or rescue from danger.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) When a distressed person cries for help and you can assist.
C) If one remembers a missed prayer.
D) When the prayer time is about to end.

Key Takeaway
Cutting an obligatory prayer to save a life is wajib when a distressed person cries for help; a parent's ordinary call does not create this obligation.

Ruling on Prayer Surfaces: Ground, Carpets, and the Rag for Sujud
States permissibility of carpets and rags, and preference for ground

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prayer surfaces and identify when using a rag for sujud is permissible or disliked.

Core Idea
Praying directly on ground is best; carpets are permissible; a rag for sujud is only for necessity.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings on what surfaces are suitable for prayer and sujud. While praying on carpets is allowed, the preferred practice is to prostrate directly on the ground or natural materials to achieve true humility. The passage also addresses the use of a cloth (khirqah) to protect against heat or cold, which is permissible only when needed. A famous anecdote about Imam Abu Hanifa illustrates how local custom can affect rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of surfaces mentioned
The text distinguishes between carpets/rugs (farash, bust, lubud), natural ground (ard), and a placed rag (khirqah) for sujud. Note the ruling for each.
Carpets are permissible if the ground exists underneath; rag is permissible for excuse but disliked otherwise.
2
Understand the preferred surface
The best is to pray on the ground without a barrier, or on plant-based mats (hasir, hashish). This is because it is closer to humility (tawadu').
Natural materials are favored over manufactured ones.
3
Analyze the condition for using a rag
Placing a rag is allowed to avoid heat, cold, or harmful roughness. For other reasons it is disliked. Also, if the rag is used to wipe used water, it becomes disliked due to impurity considerations.
The condition is 'ضرورة' (necessity) for the excuse.
4
Examine the anecdote of Imam Abu Hanifa
A man from Khwarazm criticized Abu Hanifa for using a rag. The Imam explained that in Khwarazm grass mats are used and considered permissible, while a rag is disliked—showing that local practice can influence secondary rulings.
The story highlights the principle that rulings may vary with custom (urf).
5
Compare with other schools
Imam Malik considers sujud on wool, cotton, or linen as makruh. The Hanafi position allows manufacturing materials but prefers natural ground.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different interpretations of humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بأس بالصلاة على الفرش والبسط واللبود) إذا وجد الأرض ولا بوضع خرقة يسجد عليها اتقاء الحر والبرد والخشونة الضارة (والأفضل الصلاة على الأرض) بلا حائل أو على ما تنبته كالحصير والحشيش في المساجد وهو أولى من البسط لقربه من التواضع ولا بأس بتكرار السورة في الركعتين من النفل) لأن باب النفل أوسع، وقد ورد أنه قام بآية واحدة يكررها في تهجده وفقنا الله تعالى لمثله بمنه وكرمه . قوله: (ولا بوضع خرقة يسجد عليها وعن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله أنه فعل ذلك فمر به رجل فقال يا شيخ لا تفعل مثل هذا فإنه مكروه، فقال له الإمام من أين أنت؟ فقال : من خوارزم فقال : الله أكبر جاء التكبير من وراء يعني من الصف الأخير أي على العكس يعني يحمل علم الشريعة من هنا إلى خوارزم لا من خوارزم إلى هنا، ثم قال له : أفي مسجدكم حشيش قال : نعم قال : يجوز على الحشيش ولا يجوز على الخرقة كذا في التجنيس والظاهر أن محل عدم الكراهة إذا لم ينشف بها الأعضاء من الماء المستعمل، وإلا كره نظراً إلى الرواية بنجاسته وإن كانت غير معتمدة قوله : (اتقاء الحر الخ) ظاهره أنه يكره وضعها لغير ذلك. قوله : (القربه من التواضع) وفيه خروج عن خلاف الإمام مالك فإنه يقول بكراهة السجود على ما كان من الصوف، والقطن، والكتان كذا في الشرح قوله : (من النقل أما في الفرض فيكره إلا من عذر والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم وأستغفر الله العظيم
And there is no harm in praying on carpets, rugs, and felt, if the ground exists; and there is no harm in placing a rag to prostrate on to avoid heat, cold, and harmful roughness. And the best is to pray on the ground without a barrier, or on what it grows like mats and grass in mosques, and it is better than rugs due to being closer to humility. And there is no harm in repeating a surah in the two rak'ahs of nafl, because the door of nafl is wider, and it has been reported that he (the Prophet) stood with one verse repeating it in his tahajjud. May Allah facilitate for us the like of it by His grace and generosity. His saying: (And there is no harm in placing a rag to prostrate on) and from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, that he did that, and a man passed by him and said: O elder, do not do this, for it is disliked. So the Imam said to him: Where are you from? He said: From Khwarazm. He said: Allahu Akbar, the takbir came from behind, meaning from the last row, i.e., the opposite, meaning that the knowledge of Sharia is carried from here to Khwarazm, not from Khwarazm to here. Then he said to him: Is there grass in your mosque? He said: Yes. He said: It is permissible on grass and not permissible on the rag. Thus in al-Tajnīs. And it appears that the place of no dislike is when the limbs are not wiped with it from used water, otherwise it is disliked, considering the narration of its impurity even if not relied upon. His saying: (Avoidance of heat, etc.) apparently, that it is disliked to place it for other than that. His saying: (Closer to humility) and in it there is departure from the disagreement of Imam Malik, for he says it is disliked to prostrate on what is made of wool, cotton, and linen. Thus in the sharh. His saying: (From nafl) but in fard it is disliked except for an excuse. And Allah, glorified and exalted, knows best, and I seek forgiveness from Allah, the Mighty.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi explains rulings on what surfaces are permissible and best for prayer, and the permissibility of repeating the same surah in nafl prayer. The base text states that praying on carpets, rugs, and felt is permissible when the ground is present, and placing a rag for sujud to avoid heat, cold, or harmful roughness is also permitted. However, the best is to pray directly on the ground or on natural plant materials like mats and grass, as this is closer to humility. The hashiya (commentary) expands with a story about Imam Abu Hanifa who placed a rag for sujud; a man from Khwarazm criticized it, but Abu Hanifa explained that in Khwarazm they consider grass permissible and the rag disliked, while in his region the opposite holds—showing that local practice can affect rulings. The commentary also clarifies that the rag is only disliked if it is used to wipe off used water (considered impure in some narrations). It then notes that repeating a surah in nafl is permissible, as the door of nafl is wide, and the Prophet himself repeated a single verse in tahajjud. For fard prayer, repeating a surah is disliked unless there is a reason.

Key Terms
خرقة (khirqah): A piece of cloth placed under the forehead during sujud to avoid heat, cold, or roughness
تواضع (tawāḍu'): Humility; the reason why praying directly on ground is preferred

Key Takeaway
The best surface for sujud is the ground; carpets are permissible, and a rag is only allowed for necessity, not as a regular habit.

Repeating the Same Surah in Nafl and Fard Prayers
Ruling on repeating surah in nafl and fard

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on repeating a surah in both nafl and fard prayers and identify the basis for the distinction.

Core Idea
Repeating a surah is allowed in nafl but disliked in fard, because nafl has wider scope.

This lesson addresses the ruling on reciting the same surah in both rak'ahs of a prayer. According to the Hanafi school, it is perfectly permissible in nafl (supererogatory) prayers, as the door of nafl is wide and the Prophet himself repeated a single verse in tahajjud. However, in fard (obligatory) prayers, repeating a surah is disliked (makruh) unless there is an excuse. This distinction is based on the nature of fard being more formal and requiring diversity in recitation to avoid monotony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its two contexts
The act is reciting the same surah in both rak'ahs of a prayer. The contexts are nafl and fard. The ruling differs.
The text explicitly says 'nafl' and then comments 'in fard it is disliked'.
2
Understand the ruling for nafl
Repeating a surah in nafl is permissible (la ba'sa). The reason given: 'because the door of nafl is wider' and the Prophet repeated a single verse in his tahajjud.
This indicates leniency due to the non-obligatory nature of nafl.
3
Understand the ruling for fard
The hashiya adds: 'in fard it is disliked except for an excuse'. The base text only mentions nafl; the comment extends to fard.
Dislike is tanzihi (not severely disliked) and can be waived due to necessity.
4
Examine the evidence (dalil)
The proof for permissibility in nafl is the action of the Prophet who stood with one verse repeating it in tahajjud. This shows that repetition is allowed in voluntary prayers.
The hadith is not cited in full, but referenced.
5
Note the exception for fard
The text says 'except for an excuse'. An excuse could be that the person only knows one surah or forgets others.
This prevents unnecessary hardship.

Obligation and Permission to Cut Prayer for Emergency and Parents
States when cutting prayer is obligatory or prohibited for parents' call

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when cutting a fard prayer is obligatory and when it is prohibited, and distinguish between the cases of a distressed person's cry for help and a parent's call without emergency.

Core Idea
Cutting prayer is obligatory to save life, but prohibited for a mere parental call without emergency.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on cutting a valid prayer (even fard) to respond to emergency situations. The source passage distinguishes between an obligatory duty to help a distressed person and a prohibition on breaking prayer for a parent's ordinary call. We will also explore the subtle exceptions for nafl prayers and the unique case of answering the Prophet ﷺ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage discusses cutting (qat') a prayer that is already in progress. Determine the default ruling: cutting a fard prayer is normally prohibited except for a valid excuse.
The ruling changes based on the reason for cutting.
2
Analyze the first exception: istighathah (cry for help)
When a distressed person cries for help (mustaghath), cutting becomes obligatory (wajib) even if the prayer is fard, provided the rescuer is able to help. This includes cases of oppression, drowning, or animal attack.
The obligation remains even if one fears the prayer time will expire (by analogy to turning from qiblah).
3
Analyze the second case: parents' call without emergency
If a parent calls without a cry for help, cutting the prayer is not obligatory—rather, it is prohibited (haram). The hashiya clarifies that 'not obligatory' here means 'forbidden'.
For nafl prayer, if the parent knows the child is praying, answering is preferable; if the parent does not know, answering becomes obligatory.
4
Examine the special case of the Prophet ﷺ
Answering the call of the Prophet ﷺ during prayer is obligatory, but there is disagreement on whether this invalidates the prayer.
Al-‘Aynī and Abū al-Su‘ūd note the ikhtilaf.
5
Synthesize the legal principles
The key principle: cutting prayer is only permitted for necessity (darurah). A distressed person's call constitutes darurah; a parent's ordinary call does not. The mufta bihi is that cutting for istighathah is obligatory and for parental call without distress is haram.
The hadith 'Help your brother, whether oppressor or oppressed' supports the obligation to save life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يوجب قطع الصلاة وما يجيزه وغير ذلك من تأخير الصلاة وتركها (يجب قطع الصلاة ولو فرضاً (باستغاثة) شخص (ملهوف) لمهم أصابه كما لو تعلق به ظالم أو وقع في ماء أوصال عليه حيوان فاستغاث (بالمصلي) أو بغيره وقدر على الدفع عنه ولا يجب قطع الصلاة (بنداء أحد أبويه) من غير استغاثة لأن قطع الصلاة لا يجوز إلا لضرورة وقال الطحاوي هذا في الفرض وإن كان في نافلة إن علم أحد أبويه أنه في الصلاة، وناداه لا بأس بأن لا يجيبه، وإن لم يعلم يجيبه . قوله: (أو) صال عليه حيوان أي وثب عليه . قوله: (وقدر على الدفع وإلا حرم القطع لعدم الفائدة قال بعض الفضلاء، وظاهره وجوب القطع ولو خاف خروج الوقت أخذاً من مسئلة القبلة قوله : (من غير استغاثة) فحكم الأبوين عنده يحبس حينئذ كغيرهما. قوله : (لأن قطع الصلاة لا يجوز أفهم هذا أن قول المصنف، ولا يجب قطع الصلاة المراد منه أنه يحرم عليه القطع. قوله : لا بأس بأن لا يجيبه) أفاد بلا بأس أن الأولى الإجابة عند العلم. قوله : ( يجيبه) أي وجوباً . فرع يفترض على المصلي إجابة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم واختلف في بطلانها حينئذ كذا ذكره البدر العيني، وكذا أبو السعود في تفسير سورة الأنفال.
Chapter on what necessitates cutting prayer and what permits it, and other matters of delaying prayer and abandoning it. (Cutting prayer is obligatory, even if fard, due to the cry for help of a distressed person, for a matter that befell him, such as if an oppressor seizes him, or he falls into water, or an animal attacks him, and he seeks help from the one praying or from others, and he is able to repel it. And cutting prayer is not obligatory due to the call of either of his parents without a cry for help, because cutting prayer is not permissible except for a necessity. And al-Tahawi said: This applies to fard; if it is nafl, if one of his parents knows he is in prayer and calls him, there is no harm in not answering, but if he does not know, he answers. His saying: (or) an animal pounces on him, i.e., leaps upon him. His saying: (and is able to repel) otherwise cutting is forbidden due to lack of benefit. Some virtuous scholars said: and apparently, cutting is obligatory even if he fears the time will exit, based on the issue of the qibla. His saying: (without a cry for help) so the ruling of the parents is that he is detained then like others. His saying: (because cutting prayer is not permissible) this understands that the author's statement 'and cutting prayer is not obligatory' means that it is prohibited for him to cut. His saying: (there is no harm in not answering) indicates that the preferable is to answer when knowing. His saying: (he answers) i.e., obligatorily. A branch: it is obligatory upon the one praying to answer the Prophet, peace be upon him, and there is disagreement on its invalidity then, as mentioned by Badr al-‘Aynī, and also Abū al-Su‘ūd in the commentary of Surah al-Anfāl.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This section from the chapter on cutting prayer addresses two scenarios. First, it is obligatory (fard) to cut even a fard prayer to help a distressed person who is being oppressed, drowning, or attacked by an animal, provided the person praying is able to help. The hashiya adds that if one cannot help, cutting is forbidden because it is futile. It also notes that the obligation stands even if one fears missing the prayer time, analogized to turning away from the qibla for necessity. Second, cutting prayer is not obligatory for a parent’s call unless it is an emergency; in fact, it is prohibited to cut for a mere call without distress. For nafl prayers, if the parent knows the child is praying, it is better to answer; if not, it is obligatory to answer. The commentary further discusses the special case of answering the Prophet (peace be upon him) during prayer, which is obligatory but its effect on validity is disputed.

Key Terms
استغاثة (istighāthah): A cry for help from a distressed person in serious danger
ملهوف (malhūf): A person who is distressed and seeking help
ضرورة (ḍarūrah): Necessity that permits cutting prayer

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this lesson, what is the ruling on cutting a fard prayer when a parent calls you without an apparent emergency?
A) It is obligatory to cut the prayer
B) It is permissible but not recommended
C) It is prohibited (haram) to cut the prayer
D) It is recommended to cut if the parent is angry

Key Takeaway
Cutting a fard prayer is only permissible for a genuine necessity (darurah); a parent's ordinary call does not justify it, but a cry for help makes it obligatory.

Permissible Reasons to Cut Prayer: Theft, Fear, and Midwife
Commentary on permissible reasons to cut prayer, focusing on threshold conditions and midwife scenario.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissible reasons for cutting prayer in the Hanafi school and identify the threshold conditions for property and life.

Core Idea
Cutting prayer is permitted only for weighty reasons: fear of losing property above a da’iq, or fear of harm to life, and becomes obligatory when harm is certain.

This lesson examines the hashiya's refinement of the base text on cutting prayer during salah. The commentary clarifies the minimum threshold for property loss (a da’iq, not a full dirham) and expands the scenarios to include fear for livestock, a disbeliever seeking Islam, and the midwife’s duty. Understanding these conditions is crucial for correctly applying the ruling in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling category
Determine what act is being discussed (cutting prayer) and its default ruling (generally prohibited). Then note that the text lists exceptions where it becomes mubah (permissible).
The default is that cutting prayer is haram unless one of these reasons exists.
2
Distinguish permissibility from obligation
The text states that if harm is certain (ghalabat al-zann), cutting prayer becomes wajib (obligatory). Understand the shift from mubah to wajib based on the level of certainty.
The key is ghalabat al-zann; mere possibility is not enough.
3
Analyze the property threshold
For theft, the commentary restricts the permissibility to property worth at least a da’iq (1/6 dirham). Less than that does not permit cutting prayer. Identify the source of this threshold.
The da’iq is a small weight, highlighting that only significant loss justifies interruption.
4
Examine the other scenarios
List the other reasons: fear for a child’s safety, a boiling pot, a disbeliever asking about Islam, a wolf attacking livestock, a blind person falling into a well. Understand that these involve potential harm to life or limb.
Each scenario shares the element of preventing harm, which is a higher priority than prayer.
5
Apply to the midwife case
The midwife may delay or cut prayer if she fears for the mother or child. The mother has equal right. This illustrates the concept of overriding necessity in Hanafi fiqh.
This is a practical scenario where cutting prayer is not only permitted but often required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (تخشى على ما يساوي درهماً الأولى حذف تخشى لأنه يقتضي أن الحكم غير ذلك عند تحقق السرقة مع أنه كذلك ولذا لم يأت بهذه الزيادة في الشرح والسيد قوله : (لأنه : في دائق) ظاهر التقييد أنه لا يباح قطع الصلاة ولا الحبس لما دون الدائق الحقارته. أفاده بعض الأفاضل، وفي المصباح الدائق معرب وهو سدس ،الدرهم، والدرهم الإسلامي ست عشرة حبة خرنوب والدائق حبتا ،خرنوب وثلثا حبة وكسر النون أفصح من فتحها اهـ. قوله: (وكذا لو فارت (قدرها لو قال القدر ليعم ما إذا كان ما فيه لزوجها لكان أعم فإن الظاهر أن الحكم واحد، أو الإضافة لأدنى ملابسة، ويحرر قوله : (أو خافت على ولدها) أي أن يحصل له ألم من نحو صياح قوله : (أو طلب منه كافر (الخ) إنما أبيح له البقاء في الصلاة لتعارض عبادتين، ولا يعد بذلك راضياً ببقائه على الكفر بخلاف ما إذا أخره عن الإسلام، وهو في غير الصلاة قوله (ونحوه) كأسد قوله (ونحوها كبقر قوله: (وهو كما إذا خافت الخ) أي الوجوب عند غلبة السقوط كالوجوب فيما إذا خافت القابلة الخ. قوله : (تتلقى الولد) وتقبله فمن هنا سميت القابلة قوله : (وإلا فلا بأس بتأخيرها الصلاة) أي أن لا يغلب على ظنها ما ذكر فلا بأس بتأخيرها الصلاة قوله : (وتقبل على الولد ومثلها الأم فلا وجه لمن أوجب عليها الصلاة، ولو بتيمم ولو بحفر حقيرة تضع فيها رأس المولود النازل لأن الأم أولى
His saying: (fears for what is worth a dirham) it is better to delete 'fears' because it implies that the ruling is otherwise when theft is realized, while it is the same. Therefore this addition was not brought in the sharh and by al-Sayyid. His saying: (because he is in da’iq) The apparent restriction is that cutting prayer and detention are not permitted for less than a da’iq due to its insignificance. Some virtuous scholars said that. In al-Misbah: al-da’iq is an arabicized word, and it is one-sixth of a dirham; the Islamic dirham is sixteen kharṇūb seeds; the da’iq is two and two-thirds kharṇūb seeds; breaking the nun is more eloquent than opening it. End. His saying: (and likewise if her pot boils) if he said 'pot' to include when its contents belong to her husband, it would be more general, for apparently the ruling is one, or the addition is for minimal connection; it needs editing. His saying: (or she fears for her child) i.e., that he will suffer pain from something like screaming. His saying: (or a disbeliever asks him to present Islam to him) He is only permitted to remain in prayer because of the conflict of two acts of worship, and he is not thereby considered to be pleased with his remaining on disbelief, unlike if he delayed it from Islam while not in prayer. His saying: (and similar) like a lion. His saying: (and similar to them like cows). His saying: (and it is like when she fears) i.e., the obligation when falling is dominant, like the obligation when the midwife fears. His saying: (she receives the child) and accepts it, hence she is called qābilah. His saying: (and otherwise there is no harm in her delaying the prayer) i.e., if what was mentioned is not dominant in her thought, then there is no harm in her delaying the prayer. His saying: (and she attends to the child) and the mother is like her, so there is no justification for whoever obliges her to pray, even with tayammum, and even if she digs a small pit to put the head of the descending newborn, because the mother has more right.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage from the hashiya discusses various permissible reasons to cut prayer as per the Hanafi school. The commentary refines the base text's rulings. Permissible reasons include: fear of losing property worth a dirham or more (but not less than a da’iq, i.e., 1/6 dirham); a pot boiling over (even if the contents belong to the husband); fear for the safety of one's child (e.g., from pain due to crying); a disbeliever asking to be presented with Islam (remaining in prayer is permitted because of conflicting acts of worship, but not if the delay happens outside prayer); fear of a wolf or similar predator on livestock; fear that a blind person or similar may fall into a well or the like. If it is certain (ghalabat al-zann) that harm will occur, then cutting prayer becomes obligatory. The midwife scenario: if she fears for the life of the child or mother, she must delay prayer or cut it; otherwise, she may attend to the birth without delaying prayer. The mother has the same right as the midwife. The commentary refines these points, emphasizing that the permissibility depends on the degree of fear and the value involved.

Key Terms
دائق (dā’iq): A unit of weight: 1/6 of an Islamic dirham, approximately 2/3 of a kharṇūb seed
قابلة (qābilah): Midwife who assists in childbirth
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-ẓann): Predominant certainty that harm will occur; triggers obligation to cut prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the lesson, what is the minimum value of property that permits cutting prayer due to theft?
A) Any amount
B) One dirham
C) One da’iq (1/6 dirham)
D) Ten dirhams

Key Takeaway
Cutting prayer during salah is reserved for significant reasons; the Hanafi school sets a clear property threshold (da’iq) and elevates the ruling to obligation when harm is certain.

Cutting Prayer: General Rule and Exception for Nafila
General ruling and exception for nafila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting prayer, distinguish between obligatory and supererogatory prayers regarding permissibility, and apply the condition of the parent's knowledge.

Core Idea
Cutting prayer is haram except for necessity; supererogatory prayer allows more flexibility with parents.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi delineates the general rule on cutting prayer, then refines it with an important exception for supererogatory prayers and a nuanced ruling regarding responding to parents. Understanding the layers of this ruling—from the general prohibition to the specific situations—is essential for proper practice and reflects the precision of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Locate the statement 'قطع الصلاة لا يجوز إلا لضرورة'. This is the default: cutting off prayer is not permissible except for necessity.
The word 'لا يجوز' here means haram as clarified in the hashya.
2
Distinguish Obligatory vs. Supererogatory
Al-Tahawi specifies that the rule applies to obligatory prayers. For supererogatory prayers, there is more flexibility regarding responding to a parent.
This distinction is critical for applying the ruling correctly.
3
Analyze the Parent's Knowledge Condition
If a parent knows the child is praying and calls, the child may choose not to answer without sin (la ba'sa), but it is better to answer. If the parent does not know, the child must answer (wajib).
The hashya uses 'لا بأس' to indicate permissibility, not obligation, for the first case.
4
Refine the Meaning of 'لا يجوز'
The hashya explains that the author's phrase 'لا يجب قطع الصلاة' actually means it is haram to cut, not simply 'not obligatory'.
This clarification prevents misunderstanding: what is prohibited is the act of cutting, not an obligation to refrain.
5
Note the Branch on Responding to the Prophet
It is obligatory to answer the Prophet (peace be upon him) if he calls during prayer, and there is ikhtilaf on whether that invalidates the prayer.
This shows a hierarchy: the right of the Prophet takes precedence over parents in obligation, but the effect on prayer validity is debated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطع الصلاة لا يجوز إلا لضرورة وقال الطحاوي هذا في الفرض وإن كان في نافلة إن علم أحد أبويه أنه في الصلاة، وناداه لا بأس بأن لا يجيبه، وإن لم يعلم يجيبه 
لأن قطع الصلاة لا يجوز أفهم هذا أن قول المصنف، ولا يجب قطع الصلاة المراد منه أنه يحرم عليه القطع. قوله : لا بأس بأن لا يجيبه) أفاد بلا بأس أن الأولى الإجابة عند العلم. قوله : ( يجيبه) أي وجوباً . فرع يفترض على المصلي إجابة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم واختلف في بطلانها حينئذ كذا ذكره البدر العيني، وكذا أبو السعود في تفسير سورة الأنفال.
Cutting off prayer is not permissible except for necessity. Al-Tahawi said: This is for obligatory prayer; if it is supererogatory, if one of his parents knows he is in prayer and calls him, there is no harm in not answering, but if he does not know, he answers. Because cutting off prayer is not permissible, understand from this that the author's statement 'and it is not obligatory to cut off prayer' means that it is prohibited for him to cut. His statement: 'there is no harm in not answering' indicates by 'no harm' that answering is preferable when aware. His statement: 'he answers' meaning obligatorily. A branch: It is obligatory on the praying person to answer the Prophet, peace be upon him, and there is disagreement on its nullification then, as mentioned by Badr al-Ayni and Abu al-Saud in the interpretation of Surah al-Anfal.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the general ruling: cutting off prayer is not permissible except for necessity. The commentator then clarifies that this applies to obligatory prayers. For supererogatory prayers, there is a nuance: if a parent knows the child is praying and calls, it is not blameworthy to not answer, but it is better to answer. If the parent is unaware, the child must answer. The hashya refines the matn's wording, explaining that 'does not become obligatory to cut' means it is actually prohibited to cut. It also discusses the obligation to answer the Prophet if he calls, and the differing views on whether the prayer becomes invalidated in that case.

Key Terms
قطع الصلاة (qat' al-salat): Intentionally ending the prayer before completion.
نافلة (nafilah): Supererogatory or voluntary prayer.
لا بأس (la ba'sa): No harm, meaning it is permissible but not recommended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on cutting off an obligatory prayer without necessity?
A) It is makruh tanzihi
B) It is haram
C) It is permissible
D) It is wajib

Key Takeaway
Cutting prayer is generally haram except for necessity; for supererogatory prayers, the obligation to answer a parent depends on the parent's knowledge.

Cutting Prayer to Protect Property: The Core Ruling
Ruling with proof from hadith, listing permissible grounds for cutting prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting prayer to protect property, identify the minimum threshold (daiq), and explain the proof from the hadith 'Fight to protect your property'.

Core Idea
Cutting prayer is permitted to save property worth a daiq or more, based on the hadith 'Fight to protect your property'.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that permits cutting even obligatory prayer to protect property from theft. The key elements are the minimum value threshold and the prophetic proof. We will also cover related emergencies like boiling pot and fear for a child.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is cutting the prayer. The ruling is mubah (permissible) when certain conditions are met.
2
Identify the condition
The condition is theft or potential loss of property valued at least a daiq (1/6 dirham). Below that, cutting is not permitted.
The daiq is defined precisely in the hashya.
3
Locate the dalil
The hadith 'قاتل دون مالك' (Fight to protect your property) is cited as proof.
This hadith is narrated by Bukhari and Muslim.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The command to fight implies that necessary actions to save property are legitimate, including interrupting prayer.
The Hanafi school interprets the imperative as allowing cutting prayer.
5
Note the extensions
The ruling extends to other emergencies: pot boiling over, fear for child, disbeliever requesting Islam, and protecting others' property.
These are all considered cases of overriding necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز قطعها ولو كانت فرضاً بسرقة يخشى على ما يساوي درهماً لأنه ما لوقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: قاتل دون مالك وكذا فيما دونه في الأصح لأنه يحبس في دائق، وكذا لو فارت قدرها، أو خافت على ولدها أو طلب منه كافر عرض الإسلام عليه ولو كان المسروق لغيره أي غير المصلي لدفع الظلم والنهي عن المنكر 
قوله : (تخشى على ما يساوي درهماً الأولى حذف تخشى لأنه يقتضي أن الحكم غير ذلك عند تحقق السرقة مع أنه كذلك ولذا لم يأت بهذه الزيادة في الشرح والسيد قوله : (لأنه : في دائق) ظاهر التقييد أنه لا يباح قطع الصلاة ولا الحبس لما دون الدائق الحقارته. أفاده بعض الأفاضل، وفي المصباح الدائق معرب وهو سدس ،الدرهم، والدرهم الإسلامي ست عشرة حبة خرنوب والدائق حبتا ،خرنوب وثلثا حبة وكسر النون أفصح من فتحها اهـ. قوله: (وكذا لو فارت (قدرها لو قال القدر ليعم ما إذا كان ما فيه لزوجها لكان أعم فإن الظاهر أن الحكم واحد، أو الإضافة لأدنى ملابسة، ويحرر قوله : (أو خافت على ولدها) أي أن يحصل له ألم من نحو صياح قوله : (أو طلب منه كافر (الخ) إنما أبيح له البقاء في الصلاة لتعارض عبادتين، ولا يعد بذلك راضياً ببقائه على الكفر بخلاف ما إذا أخره عن الإسلام، وهو في غير الصلاة
And it is permissible to cut it, even if obligatory, for theft of something feared to be worth a dirham, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Fight to protect your property.' And likewise for less than that according to the most correct view, because he would be imprisoned for a daiq. Likewise, if her pot boils over, or she fears for her child, or a disbeliever asks him to present Islam to him. And even if the stolen property belongs to someone else, i.e., not the one praying, to repel injustice and forbid evil. His statement: 'feared to be worth a dirham'—it is better to omit 'feared' because it implies the ruling is otherwise when theft is certain, but it is the same. Hence the commentator and al-Sayyid did not add this. His statement: 'because he would be imprisoned for a daiq'—the apparent qualification is that cutting prayer and imprisonment are not permitted for less than a daiq due to its insignificance. Some scholars have stated this. In al-Misbah, 'daiq' is a loanword meaning one-sixth of a dirham; the Islamic dirham is sixteen kharruabah seeds, and the daiq is two and two-thirds kharruabah seeds. Breaking the nun is more eloquent than opening it. His statement: 'Likewise, if her pot boils over'—if he said 'the pot' to include when its contents belong to her husband, it would be more general, for the apparent ruling is one, or the addition is for minimal connection. His statement: 'or she fears for her child'—meaning that he would suffer pain from crying, etc. His statement: 'or a disbeliever asks him'—he is permitted to remain in prayer due to the conflict of two acts of worship, and he is not thereby considered pleased with his remaining in disbelief, unlike if he delayed it from Islam when not in prayer.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage deals with cutting prayer to protect property from theft. The ruling is permissive, even for obligatory prayers, if the stolen property is worth at least one dirham (approximately 2.6 grams of silver). The proof is the hadith 'Fight to protect your property'. The hashya discusses the exact value: a dirham is 16 kharruabah seeds, and a daiq (1/6 dirham) is the threshold. It also covers cutting prayer for other emergencies: a woman whose pot boils over, fear for a child, or a disbeliever requesting to be taught Islam. Importantly, the permission extends to protecting another person's property to repel injustice.

Key Terms
درهم (dirham): A silver coin; here the Islamic dirham of 16 kharruabah seeds.
دائق (daiq): One-sixth of a dirham, i.e., 2 2/3 kharruabah seeds.
قاتل دون مالك (qatil duna malik): Hadith meaning 'Fight to protect your property', indicating defensive action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Half a dirham
D) Any amount

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits cutting prayer to protect property worth a daiq or more, based on the hadith 'Fight to protect your property'.

Hashya Refinements: Clarifying the Threshold and Linguistic Points
Hashya commentary refining the matn's wording and defining technical terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the hashya's correction of the phrase 'تخشى' and understand the precise definition of daiq and its implications.

Core Idea
The hashya refines the matn by removing the word 'تخشى' to indicate certainty, and defines daiq as 1/6 dirham, below which cutting is not permitted.

This lesson delves into the author's (al-Tahtawi's) critical commentary on the matn. He points out that the phrase 'تخشى على ما يساوي درهماً' is imprecise because the ruling applies even when theft is certain, not only when feared. He also explains the exact measure of a daiq and why values below it are insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase being refined
The matn says 'تخشى على ما يساوي درهماً' (feared to be worth a dirham). The hashya says it is better to omit 'تخشى'.
The word 'تخشى' means 'feared'.
2
Understand the critique
Why omit? Because the ruling is the same whether the theft is certain or merely feared. The word 'تخشى' wrongly implies that the ruling is different when certain.
The hashya argues for unconditional application.
3
Learn the weight of daiq
The hashya clarifies that a daiq is 1/6 of a dirham, equal to 2 2/3 kharruabah seeds. Below this, cutting is not allowed due to insignificance.
The definition is from al-Misbah.
4
Note the authority
The hashya references other commentators like al-Sayyid who did not add 'تخشى'.
This shows the refinement is based on scholarly consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز قطعها ولو كانت فرضاً بسرقة يخشى على ما يساوي درهماً لأنه ما لوقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: قاتل دون مالك وكذا فيما دونه في الأصح لأنه يحبس في دائق، وكذا لو فارت قدرها، أو خافت على ولدها أو طلب منه كافر عرض الإسلام عليه ولو كان المسروق لغيره أي غير المصلي لدفع الظلم والنهي عن المنكر 
قوله : (تخشى على ما يساوي درهماً الأولى حذف تخشى لأنه يقتضي أن الحكم غير ذلك عند تحقق السرقة مع أنه كذلك ولذا لم يأت بهذه الزيادة في الشرح والسيد قوله : (لأنه : في دائق) ظاهر التقييد أنه لا يباح قطع الصلاة ولا الحبس لما دون الدائق الحقارته. أفاده بعض الأفاضل، وفي المصباح الدائق معرب وهو سدس ،الدرهم، والدرهم الإسلامي ست عشرة حبة خرنوب والدائق حبتا ،خرنوب وثلثا حبة وكسر النون أفصح من فتحها اهـ. قوله: (وكذا لو فارت (قدرها لو قال القدر ليعم ما إذا كان ما فيه لزوجها لكان أعم فإن الظاهر أن الحكم واحد، أو الإضافة لأدنى ملابسة، ويحرر قوله : (أو خافت على ولدها) أي أن يحصل له ألم من نحو صياح قوله : (أو طلب منه كافر (الخ) إنما أبيح له البقاء في الصلاة لتعارض عبادتين، ولا يعد بذلك راضياً ببقائه على الكفر بخلاف ما إذا أخره عن الإسلام، وهو في غير الصلاة
And it is permissible to cut it, even if obligatory, for theft of something feared to be worth a dirham, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Fight to protect your property.' And likewise for less than that according to the most correct view, because he would be imprisoned for a daiq. Likewise, if her pot boils over, or she fears for her child, or a disbeliever asks him to present Islam to him. And even if the stolen property belongs to someone else, i.e., not the one praying, to repel injustice and forbid evil. His statement: 'feared to be worth a dirham'—it is better to omit 'feared' because it implies the ruling is otherwise when theft is certain, but it is the same. Hence the commentator and al-Sayyid did not add this. His statement: 'because he would be imprisoned for a daiq'—the apparent qualification is that cutting prayer and imprisonment are not permitted for less than a daiq due to its insignificance. Some scholars have stated this. In al-Misbah, 'daiq' is a loanword meaning one-sixth of a dirham; the Islamic dirham is sixteen kharruabah seeds, and the daiq is two and two-thirds kharruabah seeds. Breaking the nun is more eloquent than opening it. His statement: 'Likewise, if her pot boils over'—if he said 'the pot' to include when its contents belong to her husband, it would be more general, for the apparent ruling is one, or the addition is for minimal connection. His statement: 'or she fears for her child'—meaning that he would suffer pain from crying, etc. His statement: 'or a disbeliever asks him'—he is permitted to remain in prayer due to the conflict of two acts of worship, and he is not thereby considered pleased with his remaining in disbelief, unlike if he delayed it from Islam when not in prayer.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage deals with cutting prayer to protect property from theft. The ruling is permissive, even for obligatory prayers, if the stolen property is worth at least one dirham (approximately 2.6 grams of silver). The proof is the hadith 'Fight to protect your property'. The hashya discusses the exact value: a dirham is 16 kharruabah seeds, and a daiq (1/6 dirham) is the threshold. It also covers cutting prayer for other emergencies: a woman whose pot boils over, fear for a child, or a disbeliever requesting to be taught Islam. Importantly, the permission extends to protecting another person's property to repel injustice.

Key Terms
درهم (dirham): A silver coin; here the Islamic dirham of 16 kharruabah seeds.
دائق (daiq): One-sixth of a dirham, i.e., 2 2/3 kharruabah seeds.
قاتل دون مالك (qatil duna malik): Hadith meaning 'Fight to protect your property', indicating defensive action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the exact weight of a daiq according to the hashya?
A) 2 kharruabah seeds
B) 2 2/3 kharruabah seeds
C) 3 kharruabah seeds
D) 1/16 dirham

Key Takeaway
The hashya refines the ruling by removing the condition of 'fear' and precisely defines daiq as 1/6 dirham.

Cutting Prayer for Fear of Wolf or Falling
Ruling on cutting prayer for fear of wolf or falling, with obligation if harm is strongly anticipated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting prayer for fear of harm to life or property and identify when it becomes obligatory.

Core Idea
Fear of harm permits cutting prayer; strong fear makes it wajib.

The Hanafi school addresses practical emergencies during prayer. This passage clarifies that protecting life and property can justify cutting the prayer, and when the risk is nearly certain, it becomes an obligation. We'll explore the ruling, its conditions, and the boundary between permissibility and obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is cutting (qat') the prayer. The default ruling according to the text is that it is permissible (ja'iz) when there is fear of harm to livestock or a person falling.
The text uses 'yajuz' indicating permissibility.
2
Recognize the conditions for permissibility
Permissibility applies specifically to fear of a predator (like a wolf) on sheep, or fear of a blind or unaware person falling into a well or from a roof.
The examples show the scope: property and bodily harm.
3
Identify the threshold for obligation
When the fear becomes dominant probability (ghalabat al-zann) that harm will occur, cutting the prayer becomes wajib (obligatory), even for obligatory prayers.
The text says 'وجب قطع الصلاة'.
4
Apply the rule to similar scenarios
The hashya expands 'wolf' to include any predator (e.g., lion) and 'sheep' to include other livestock (e.g., cattle). The same reasoning applies to any situation where property or life is at similar risk.
5
Distinguish permissibility from obligation
Permissibility is for lesser fear; obligation is triggered when the probability of harm is overwhelming. The key is the strength of the fear, not the type of harm alone.
Ghalabat al-zann is a strong presumption, not certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز قطعها لخشية خوف من ذئب ونحوه على غنم ونحوها أو خوف تردى أي سقوط أعمى أو غيره مما لا علم في بئر ونحوه كحفيرة وسطح، وإذا غلب على الظن سقوطه وجب قطع الصلاة ولو فرضاً 
قوله (ونحوه) كأسد قوله (ونحوها كبقر
And it is permissible to cut it due to fear of a wolf or similar for sheep and the like, or fear of falling, i.e., a blind person or other fall of one who does not know into a well or the like, such as a pit or a roof. And if it is strongly thought that he will fall, cutting the prayer becomes obligatory, even if obligatory. His statement: (and the like) like a lion. His statement: (and the like) like cattle.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage covers two scenarios where cutting prayer is permissible: protecting livestock from predators, and preventing a blind or unaware person from falling into a well or similar danger. The hashya adds examples: a lion is similar to a wolf, and cattle are similar to sheep. The key ruling is that if the likelihood of harm is dominant (ghalabat al-zann), cutting the prayer becomes wajib (obligatory), even for obligatory prayers.

Key Terms
خوف تردى (khawf taraddi): Fear of falling of a helpless person (like a blind person) into a dangerous place.
غلب على الظن (ghalaba 'ala al-zann): Dominant probability; a level of certainty that triggers obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does cutting the prayer become obligatory?
A) When there is any fear of loss
B) When there is a strong presumption (ghalabat al-zann) of harm
C) Only when the harm is certain
D) It never becomes obligatory

Key Takeaway
Cutting prayer for fear of harm is permissible; it becomes obligatory when harm is strongly anticipated (ghalabat al-zann).

Midwife Delaying Prayer to Save Child or Mother
Obligates midwife to delay or cut prayer to save child or mother

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a midwife delaying or cutting prayer to save a child or mother and identify the proof from the Prophet's action.

Core Idea
Protecting life takes precedence over prayer timing when loss is strongly feared.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi concession for a midwife (al-qabilah) who is attending a birth. The focus is on when she may delay or even cut her prayer to save the child or mother. The ruling is derived from the Prophet's own delay of prayers during the Battle of the Khandaq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the midwife is doing (delaying or cutting prayer) and the hukm level (wajib or mubah) based on the level of fear.
The ruling changes with the strength of the fear.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited in the text: the Prophet's delay of four prayers on the day of al-Khandaq.
This is a Sunnah (act of the Prophet) used as evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the Prophet's action proves the ruling: because protecting life (or dealing with a pressing need) justifies delaying the prayer.
The Prophet's delay was due to the necessity of battle; here the necessity is childbirth.
4
Note the condition
Identify the condition: 'ghalabat al-zann' (strong presumption) of death or harm to child or mother.
If the fear is not strong, delay becomes merely permissible (mubah), not obligatory.
5
Evaluate the ikhtilaf
Recognize that other schools may not allow such delay, so note the ikhtilaf and the practical difference.
The Hanafi position is based on the Prophetic precedent and prioritization of life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو كما إذا خافت القابلة وهي المرأة التي يقال لها داية تتلقى الولد حال خروجه من بطن أمه إن غلب على ظنها موت الولد أو تلف عضو منه، أو أمه بتركها وجب عليها تأخير الصلاة عن وقتها وقطعها لو كانت فيها وإلا فلا بأس بتأخيرها الصلاة وتقبل على الولد للعذر كما أخر النبي الصلاة عن وقتها يوم الخندق 
قوله: (وهو كما إذا خافت الخ) أي الوجوب عند غلبة السقوط كالوجوب فيما إذا خافت القابلة الخ. قوله: (تتلقى الولد) وتقبله فمن هنا سميت القابلة قوله : (وإلا فلا بأس بتأخيرها الصلاة) أي أن لا يغلب على ظنها ما ذكر فلا بأس بتأخيرها الصلاة قوله : (وتقبل على الولد ومثلها الأم فلا وجه لمن أوجب عليها الصلاة، ولو بتيمم ولو بحفر حقيرة تضع فيها رأس المولود النازل لأن الأم أولى 
بالتأخير من القابلة وتمامه في الشرح قوله : (كما أخر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الصلاة) أي جنسها فإنّ المشركين شغلوه عن أربع صلوات فقضاهن مرتباً الظهر، ثم العصر ثم المغرب، ثم العشاء.
And it is like when the midwife fears—she is the woman called al-dayyah who receives the child during its emergence from the mother's womb: if she strongly thinks the child will die or a limb of it will be lost, or the mother will die by leaving her, it becomes obligatory for her to delay the prayer from its time and to cut it if she is in it. Otherwise, there is no harm in her delaying the prayer and attending to the child due to the excuse, just as the Prophet, peace be upon him, delayed the prayer from its time on the day of al-Khandaq. His statement: (and it is like when she fears) i.e., the obligation when a fall is dominant is like the obligation when the midwife fears. His statement: (she receives the child) and accepts him, thus she is called al-qabilah. His statement: (otherwise there is no harm in her delaying the prayer) i.e., if she does not strongly think what was mentioned, then there is no harm in her delaying the prayer. His statement: (and she attends to the child) and the mother is like her, so there is no justification for those who make prayer obligatory upon her, even by tayammum, even by digging a small hole to place the head of the descending newborn, because the mother has more right. With the delay from the midwife and its completion in the sharh. His statement: (just as the Prophet delayed the prayer) i.e., its type; the polytheists occupied him from four prayers, so he made them up in order: Zuhr, then Asr, then Maghrib, then Isha.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling for a midwife who is assisting a birth. If she strongly fears that the child or mother will die or be harmed if she leaves to pray, it becomes obligatory to delay the prayer (if still within its time) or to cut the prayer (if she is already in it). If there is no such strong fear, it is permissible (no harm) to delay the prayer to attend to the birth. The proof is the Prophet's own delay of prayers during the Battle of the Khandaq due to conflict. The hashya notes that the mother herself is also allowed to delay prayer for childbirth, contrary to some who would require her to pray even with tayammum.

Key Terms
قابلة (qabilah): Midwife; a woman who assists childbirth.
تخلف الصلاة (takhlif al-salah): Delaying the prayer from its prescribed time due to an excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When must a midwife delay or cut her prayer according to the text?
A) Whenever she is attending a birth
B) If she strongly fears death or harm to child or mother
C) Only if the mother requests it
D) Never; she must always pray on time

Key Takeaway
Protecting life through strong fear of harm makes delaying or cutting prayer wajib in Hanafi fiqh, based on the Prophet's precedent.

Delaying Obligatory Prayer for a Valid Excuse
Permits delaying prayer for fear of harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delaying obligatory prayer due to fear of harm and apply the qiyas deriving this permission from the combatant's excuse.

Core Idea
Fear of losing life or livelihood permits delaying prayer; necessity overrides timeliness.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses a practical scenario: a traveler in open space fears thieves, wild animals, or floods. It rules that he may delay the obligatory prayer, just as combatants in battle may delay. This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy), balancing two competing obligations: prayer on time vs. protection from imminent harm. The text also extends this logic to residents and includes nuances on making up missed prayers (qada) and other devotional acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Locate what action is being ruled upon: delaying an obligatory prayer (salat al-waqtiyya) for a traveler (musafir) who fears thieves, highwaymen, wild beasts, or flood. The ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) to delay.
The term 'musafir' here is linguistic, not the shari'i traveler who shortens prayers.
2
Locate the Dalil – Qiyas
The proof is an analogy (qiyas) to combatants (muqatilin) who cannot dismount to pray during battle. They are permitted to delay because engaging in fighting is a necessity that cannot be made up, whereas prayer can be made up (qada). The traveler's case shares the same effective cause (illa): fear of losing a necessity (safety) that cannot be made up.
The illa is 'impossibility of making up the missed benefit' (ta'addur al-tadarruk).
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The source text states: 'like the combatants if they cannot gesture while riding due to the excuse.' The link is that both involve a genuine excuse (udhr) that makes timely performance harmful. The ruling of permissibility to delay transfers from combatants to travelers via qiyas.
This is a qiyas jaliy because the illa is clearly shared.
4
Note the Scope and Ikhtilaf
The hashya extends the ruling to a resident (muqim) as well, indicating the excuse is the determining factor. The passage also mentions a difference of opinion regarding the timing of sajdat al-tilawa and nadhr mutabiq: some scholars (al-Tahawi) hold it is expansive (musa'), others (al-Hilwani, al-Amiri) hold it is restricted (mudayyiq).
This ikhtilaf is intra-Hanafi and also applies to qada of Ramadan.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that delaying obligatory prayer is permissible when there is a valid excuse, whether for a traveler or resident. The qada of missed prayers, though normally immediate, may also be delayed with a valid excuse like earning for dependents.
This is supported by the zahir al-riwayah and later fatwa practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا المسافر أي السائر في فضاء إذا خاف من اللصوص، أو قطاع الطريق أو من سبع أو سيل جاز له تأخير الوقتية كالمقاتلين إذا لم يقدروا على الإيماء ركباناً للعذر وكذا يجوز تأخير قضاء الفوائت للعذر كالسعي على العيال، وإن وجب قضاؤها على الفور وأما قضاء الصوم فعلى التراخي ما لم يقرب رمضان الثاني، وأما سجدة التلاوة والنذر المطابق ففيهما الخلاف قيل موسع وقيل مضيق 
قوله : (أي السائر في فضاء أفاد به أنّ المراد السفر اللغوي، ومثله فيما يظهر ليس بقيد بل كذلك المقيم. قوله: (كالمقاتلين إذا لم يقدروا الخ) لأنهم إذا فاتهم القتال بالإشتغال بالصلاة لا يمكنهم تداركه والصلاة يمكنهم تدارك ما فات منها قوله : قيل موسع) قائله الطحاوي. قوله: (وقيل مضيق) قائله الحلواني ،والعامري، وهذا الخلاف يجري في قضاء رمضان كما في الدر.
And likewise the traveler, i.e., one walking in open space, if he fears thieves or highwaymen or a wild beast or a flood, it is permissible for him to delay the timely prayer like the combatants if they cannot gesture while riding due to the excuse. Likewise, it is permissible to delay the qada of missed prayers due to an excuse, like earning a living for dependents, even though their qada is obligatory immediately. As for the qada of fasting, it is at leisure as long as the next Ramadan does not approach. As for the prostration of recitation and an unrestricted vow, there is a difference of opinion: some say it is expanded, some say it is restricted. His statement: (i.e., one walking in open space) indicates that the intended meaning is linguistic travel, and likewise it appears not to be a condition, rather the resident is also the same. His statement: (like the combatants if they cannot...) because if they miss fighting by occupying themselves with prayer, they cannot make it up, while prayer they can make up what they missed. His statement: (it is said expanded) its proponent is al-Tahawi. His statement: (and it is said restricted) its proponent is al-Hilwani and al-Amiri, and this difference also applies to the qada of Ramadan as in al-Durr.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage extends the ruling to a traveler who is in open space and fears thieves, highwaymen, predators, or floods. He may delay the obligatory prayer, just as combatants who cannot dismount to pray may delay. The proof is the analogy: in both cases, engaging in the immediate necessity (fighting or escaping) takes precedence. The text also discusses qada: although missed prayers must be made up immediately, if there is an excuse like earning for dependents, it can be delayed. Fasting, however, is more flexible. The hashya clarifies that this applies to residents too, not just travelers.

Key Terms
مسافر (musafir): Traveler; here defined as one walking in open space (sair fi fada'), not necessarily reaching travel distances for qasr.
قضاء الفوائت (qada' al-fawa'it): Making up missed obligatory prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the qada of missed prayers when one has an excuse like earning for dependents?
A) Must be done immediately before any other action
B) Can be delayed due to the excuse
C) It is waived entirely
D) Only permissible if one is traveling

Key Takeaway
Necessity permits delaying obligatory prayers and their makeup, as long as the excuse is genuine and the prayer is eventually performed.

Punishment for Intentionally Leaving Prayer
Prescribes beating and imprisonment for one who abandons prayer out of laziness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the worldly punishment for abandoning prayer out of laziness and identify the dalil used.

Core Idea
Abandoning prayer out of laziness earns severe beating and imprisonment, not disbelief—a ta'zir punishment to compel prayer.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses a critical question: what happens to a Muslim who intentionally abandons prayer out of laziness? The Hanafi school prescribes a severe but non-lethal punishment aimed at bringing the person back to prayer. We will examine the ruling, its conditions, and the proof from the hadith of Jabir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what exactly is being ruled upon (abandoning prayer out of laziness) and what the prescribed punishment is (beating and imprisonment).
The ruling distinguishes between abandoning prayer due to laziness vs. denial.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: the hadith of Jabir, 'Between a man and disbelief is abandoning prayer.' Understand its status as ahad and its implication.
The hadith is used to show gravity, not to declare kufr.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith proves the punishment. The severe warning justifies a severe worldly penalty to prevent the sin.
The hadith's apparent threat is interpreted as a warning for the punishment, not literal kufr.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Observe the difference of opinion on the interpretation of 'ghayy' in the Quran and the general disagreement with other schools on the ruling.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih; Shafi'i and Hanbali hold different views (not detailed here).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi position
Summarize why the Hanafi school adopts ta'zir punishment rather than hadd, and note the practical implications for the ruler.
The punishment is discretionary (ta'zir), not a fixed hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتارك الصلاة عمداً كسلاً يضرب ضرباً شديداً حتى يسيل منه الدم و بعده يحبس ولا يترك هملا، بل يتفقد حاله بالوعظ والزجر والضرب أيضاً حتى يصليها أو يموت بحبسه وهذا جزاؤه الدنيوي، وأما في الآخرة إذا مات على الإسلام عاصياً بتركها فله عذاب طويل بواد في جهنم أشدها حراً، وأبعدها قعراً فيه بئر يقال له الهبهب، وآبار يسيل إليها الصديد، والقيح، أعدت لتارك الصلاة، وحديث جابر فيه صفته بقوله: بين الرجل وبين الكفر ترك الصلاة رواه أحمد ومسلم 
قوله : ( وتارك الصلاة عمداً كسلاً) احترز به عن الترك سهواً، أو لعذر فليس عليه شيء مما ذكر قوله : ( وآبار الخ الواو بمعني، أو وهي لحكاية الخلاف فإنهم اختلفوا في تفسير الغي في قوله تعالى : فسوف يلقون عيا فقيل الضلال، وقال الحسن عذاباً طويلاً، وقال ابن عباس شراً وقيل آبار في جهنم الخ أفاده في الشرح قوله : ( وحديث جابر) مبتدأ خبره قوله فيه صفته أي صفة تارك الصلاة.
And the one who abandons prayer intentionally out of laziness is beaten severely until blood flows, and thereafter imprisoned, and not left neglected, but his condition is monitored with exhortation, reprimand, and also beating until he prays or dies in his imprisonment. This is his worldly punishment. As for the Hereafter, if he dies as a Muslim disobedient by abandoning it, he will have a long punishment in a valley in Hell, the hottest, deepest, containing a well called al-Habhab, and wells into which pus and pus fluid flows, prepared for the abandoner of prayer. The hadith of Jabir describes him: 'Between a man and disbelief is abandoning prayer' narrated by Ahmad and Muslim. His statement: (And the one who abandons prayer intentionally out of laziness) excludes accidental abandonment or due to an excuse, so nothing of what is mentioned applies to him. His statement: (and wells) the waw means 'or', and it is for relating the difference of opinion, for they differed on the interpretation of 'ghayy' in the verse 'Then they will meet ghayy'. It is said it means error, al-Hasan said a long punishment, Ibn Abbas said evil, and it is said wells in Hell, as stated in the sharh. His statement: (the hadith of Jabir) the subject, its predicate is 'in it is his description', i.e., the description of the abandoner of prayer.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage discusses the punishment for a person who omits prayer deliberately out of laziness (not denial). The worldly punishment is severe beating until blood flows, followed by imprisonment until he prays or dies. He is not left alone but is constantly reminded and disciplined. The afterlife punishment is described vividly: a valley in Hell with a well called al-Habhab. The proof is the hadith of Jabir which states that abandoning prayer is the line between a person and disbelief. The hashya clarifies that accidental omission or forgetfulness is excused.

Key Terms
تارك الصلاة كسلاً (tarik al-salat kasalan): One who omits prayer out of laziness, not denial of its obligation.
ضرب شديد (darb shadid): Severe beating until blood flows, as ta'zir punishment.
الغي (al-ghayy): A word from Quran 19:59 interpreted variously as 'error', 'evil', or a valley in Hell.
الهبهب (al-habhab): A well in Hell prepared for those who abandon prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No punishment if he repents

Key Takeaway
Severe ta'zir punishment awaits the one who abandons prayer out of laziness, reflecting the highest importance of salah in Islam.
